Arabic is the fifth-most-spoken language in the world, with more than 313m speakers.

Except that its fragmentation makes each dialekt marginal!!

There are three times as many articles in French on Wikipedia as in Arabic, with five times as many edits. The Arabic book market is about a quarter the size of Belgiumâ€™s.

Five approximate categories: north African, Egyptian, Mesopotamian, Levantine and Peninsular Arabic.

Arabic dialects are not mutually intelligable. MSA in the internet made things better.
Arabic can be difficult but simple. English can be easy but complex.
If in 1200 romance languages were named Neo-Latin, this language wouldn't be very mutually intelligible either.

The pattern system is two way:
m--a-a 	place name 	Ù…ÙƒØªØ¨Ø©	maktaba (library)
-aa-i- 	active participle 	ÙƒØ§ØªØ¨ 	kaatib (writer)
ma--uu- 	passive participle 	Ù…ÙƒØªÙˆØ¨	maktuub (written)
-a-a-a 	basic verb 	ÙƒØªØ¨	kataba (to write)
a--a-a 	causative verb 	Ø£ÙƒØªØ¨	aktaba (to dictate)
-i-aa- 	noun 	ÙƒØªØ§Ø¨	kitaab (book)
-u-u- 	plural noun 	ÙƒØªØ¨	kutub (books)

these transformations are super algorithmic

There are only about a total of 6000 lexical root words in Arabic, so on a computer you can represent each word by encoding it's lexical root, and the grammatical transformation on it. 

Yamli is a popular tool which automatically transliterates Arabic that has been written phonetically in Roman characters back to correctly spelled Arabic letters, so if you've heard a word but don't know how to spell it you can still Google it.

The root/pattern approach really goes crazy with verbs. There are ten common verb patterns in Arabic, and each one alters the base meaning in a semi-predictable way. you can use them to make very good guesses about new vocabulary. 

Because of this rich structure Arabic is too rigid for new vocabulary

http://idlewords.com/2011/08/why_arabic_is_terrific.htm
Broken Plurals
foot -> feet the norm
Plurals mostly change the structure of the word quite dramatically: 
kitaab 	->	kutub	(book)
ustaath 	->	asaatitha	(teacher)
maqha 	-> 	maqaahi	(cafÃ©)
dukkan 	-> 	dakaakiin	(store)
ahdar 	-> 	hudur	(green)

ÙƒÙƒÙƒ ØªØªØª Ø¹Ø¹Ø¹ Ù…Ù…Ù…

all non-human plurals are grammatically feminine singular:
al-kutub hadra' (Ø§Ù„ÙƒØªØ¨ Ø®Ø¶Ø±Ø§Ø¡)

"The books, she is green"

MSA is further from region arabic than Latin is from Spanish.

MSA is the official language of newspapers, news segments, and legal documents, governments speeches even wedding invitations and not to forget the Quran and the Arabic bible.

Egyptian being so different as to sound like a separate language.

Some scholars make the case that the dialects are not really dialects, they are their OWN LANGUAGE that were influenced by Arabic.

See for example Taleb's argument on Lebanese being a language of its own.

Arabic has 4-12 million speakers in Europe depending on how you count. If we take the most generous estimation (12 million) which compares 2nd and 3rd generation immigrants to native speakers it's at best the 13th most spoken language in Europe[2].

Historically it makes some sense because North Africa and the Laventine didn't have much in common with Arabia before Islam so they must've had their own local languages (say Berber or Aramaic) that was influenced by Arabic.

Note:
The etymologies and the question of borrowings.
Etymologies are listed in rectangular brackets.

If there are cognates of the Arabic word in the other Sem languages, the etymology will look like this: [Sem ...]

If the Arabic word is clearly a borrowing from a non-Sem language, the etymology will look like this: [from ...]

If there are no known cognates of the Arabic word in the other Sem languages, the etymology will look like this: [...] Most of the words in brackets are assumed to be intra-Sem borrowings.

If there are no known cognates of the Arabic word in either the other Sem languages or elsewhere, the etymology will look like this: [?] <removed>

Borrowings from Ara into non-Sem languages are listed after the etymology in rectangular brackets. Sources:
